http://ipt.vertnet.org:8080/ipt/resource.do?r=cuml_sound_film									urn:lsid:biocol.org:col:34939			CUML	Sound/Film			MachineObservation					125338	NOTES: 90% confidence Eulemur Rubriventer.  Locals concur that this is certainly rubriventer.  Dr. Lehman has encountered rubriventer making this sound on several occasions, he says, but the function and context surrounding this unusual call is totally unclear.  This is the only known loud call of rubriventer.  Given that they are monogamous primates and living in a fixed home range they would be expected to have a loud call of some sort.  Perhaps this unusual call relates to their unusal activity pattern: cathemeral, which is active during the day and night.  LNS NOTE: Unbroken cut with internal recordist announcement/handling noise gain change (MJA, 29 June 2005). http://ipt.vertnet.org:8080/ipt/resource.do?r=cuml_sound_film									urn:lsid:biocol.org:col:34939			CUML	Sound/Film			MachineObservation					125310	"NOTES: adult female and later an adult male  with tail-flagging periods and caller sex verbally noted on tape.   Her initial tail-flagging and associated ""rough grunts"" isfollowed by several other group members engaging in the tail-flagging display.  It is typical for flagging and ""rough-grunting"" to began with one highly aroused individual and then be followed by several or many other group members all flagging.   Tail flagging may be a mobbing display, though some researchers feel that is it more likely a ""distraction display"" (Dr. P.C. Wright, personal communication). No formal data exist to firmly answer this question.    NOTE GREAT ""SHRIEKS"" AT 6:38, 7:43, AND 8:14.  Such ""SHRIEKS""  are given  collectively by groups at dawn and dusk (in a chorus fashion) as well as by single spatially separated (""lost"") individuals.  LNS NOTE: Unbroken cut with internal recordist announcement/handling noise gain change (MJA, 29 June 2005)." http://ipt.vertnet.org:8080/ipt/resource.do?r=cuml_sound_film									urn:lsid:biocol.org:col:34939			CUML	Sound/Film			MachineObservation					125302	NOTES: grunts and sneeze-honks while foraging and light travel around dusk.  One question is why do they add the sneeze-honks at certain times in addition to the common grunts which are typically given during foraging-associated movement.  Grunts were almost never seen to be given while the rubriventers were stationary.  The sneeze-honk addition may be associated with higher arousal states.  For example, in one agonistic confrontation between a Malagasy Striped Civet (Fossa fosana) a lone rubriventer male was seen (not by myself) to emit many of the sneeze-honks and with much greater amplitude, and rate than normally (Mitchell Irwin, Anthropology Graduate Student at StoneyBrook, personal communication).
